And the reduction of the dislocation is that you place the hand joint of the patient on a table and have them extend their arm. The surgeon then places their hand or palm over the area near the joint and gently push it back into its proper location.
But firstly it should be considered if the palm is already placed on the belly of the hand, then the patient places both hands on a table for extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is previous, place both hands, then the position of the hand of the physician intrinsically on the table, so that the physician's hand falls over that vicinity of the joint.
And so if it returns soon, that is good; and if not, then strengthen the area by applying pressure to the abscess and leave it as it is; and do not repeat this, for if you do, you will neither be able nor allowed to proceed with the reduction of the dislocation for several days.
But according to the tortuosity of the joint; and do not note any cause to the patient, but rather that the hand is softened and cannot strengthen any cause; then you know that the nerves are torn or stretched, and in this case it should be strengthened with a cautery near the site and reinforce it with some cause.
And since the swelling has returned, place it on the poultice which we have recommended, and let it be applied and strengthened for five days; then loosen it and leave it, and exercise the hand.
But if the movement in it has been overstayed and some gravity occurs in it, then apply that which is water cauda and with friction several times, until it is alleviated.
Chapter XXIX.
On the cure of dislocation of the fingers.
For some time, dislocate the fingers completely.
And when one finger is dislocated on the side of the palm or in its center, then hold the finger and push the dislocation back with your fingers until it returns to place.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend that part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend that part and extend it until it is straightened in that day.
And when it is night, ligature that according to that narration, and do not cease to release and exercise it during the day. Ligature it at night; and do this for several days until it is straightened.
And make it seem as though you do this, if it is dislocated in the abdomen of the hand, and join that to that part, and do this your first operation, within which it be healed.
And make it seem as though you do this when it is dislocated in the other parts.
Capitol.xxx.
For the cure of dislocation of the cervical spine.
When the vertebrae of the back or the neck are fully dislocated, and several vertebrae around that location are displaced, then there is no cure for that, for death will ensue to the patient.
And the sign of this is that the patient's excretion is forced out against their will, and they cannot retain it; several times, parts of their body or their legs or one of their arms become soft; or a vertebra is displaced from its place, for several times are displaced and multiple divisions are made to remove those dislocations in four directions.
Then that is mended at the rear parts and is named gilblowitz.
May the cure of it be that when you consider if the gilblowitz arises from puella, then in that case there is neither cure nor does it heal completely.
May it be that in those which come from congelation or percussion or semblance to those, the Ancients have spoken of the methods of cure at length, of which the most returns to prophecy; but I will now abbreviate that of which the slight omission from excess of that, for my proximity to the clarity of my exposition, which I have here expounded and formed from various instruments, by which I will show and expand that.
And of those, only the one that arises in the two sides is cured as I advise.
And it is that the patient be extended on his back close to the wall, and be covered with a wet cloth so that his feet are not chilled.
And place a straight stake, planted in the ground at the end of the back near its head, and another stake on the sides of the feet at the other end of the stake.
The minister hold the stake, and it should not be fixed in a hole; another minister hold the other according to that form.
And wrap around the head of the patient and tie tightly with a strong linen band; extend the end of the bandage to the stake which is at the head of the patient, and tie that there; and tie the other bandage tightly around the hips of the patient under his buttocks, and support his legs; and adjust all the bandages and tie those to the other stake which supports his feet.
And the minister should extend the bandage to the part of the stake, and do not move both stakes and the bandages in which they are fixed, but they should not be fixed in this manner as we have said.
And the doctor places his palms on the spine with strength so that it returns, or he places one hand on a table and adjusts his feet on the table as it returns.
And if it does not return in this way, then take a table of which the breadth or length is about three braccia, and dig in the wall where we said was near the wound a place at the end of the table. And place the majority of the table on the spine, and the doctor places his feet on the other end. He presses well so that the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you wish, open a hole in the buttock which is revolved with your hand; and when you are on the ground, place the patient's head at the heel of two sticks, the length of which is one braccia, and place his head at the heel of two sticks in the same manner; and let the distance between each stick be a palm. Then put something through each hole to turn the spine, and secure each end firmly on the ground so that it does not move.
And there should be a round stick for the hole, and it should be turned in the two slots of the two sticks.
And at the end of it is a slot in which the stick is inserted, the length of which is one palm's breadth as it is turned; and in the other two sticks, it is similar to that.
And then strengthen the ligaments you have pulled in the feet of the patient in the slot where you place their head, and the ligaments you have pulled in the thighs of the patient in the hole where you place their feet.
And then you should place each hole for a minister to hold the hole with his hand, and the doctor straighten the spine according to what we have said.
And when the spine is returned and the area is straightened, then cover it with a paste of honey, and some wool; then place a slice of table wax over the paste, such that its width is about three fingers or nearly those, and its length is sufficient to cover the part of the spine, and on top of that portion of the spine.
And then apply the paste which we have mentioned, and use the patient for subtle nourishment until it is healed.
And if there is any danger in the area at the end of the healing, then ensure that medicines that mitigate and soothe are administered through long-term use, which we have recommended; and already a layer of lead has been applied to that location.
And sometimes accidents occur at the end of the vertebrae in the back, and are caused by that dislocation; and it is that bone where you already foresee that no suspicion should arise about it with this medicine, for it would make your accident fatal.
Capitol.xxxxi.
Of the cure for a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle should only occur through dislocation alone, and do not happen in those cases where small displacement and凹陷 occurs in other joints.
And the joint of the thigh is dislocated according to four manners, which is dislocated to the internal parts, or to the external parts, or to the anterior parts, or to the posterior parts.
And primarily it is dislocated to the internal parts, and little to the anterior or posterior parts.
And the sign of dislocation to the internal parts is that, when you adjust the healthy leg's knee against the malformed one, the malformed leg appears longer and the kneecap is more prominent than the healthy side. The malformed leg cannot bend its knee; instead, it must be stretched out. This is the place where a cauterization of the apostema is applied, so that the head of the malformation is already reached here.
And the sign of one who suffers dislocation to the external parts is that the symptoms of the condition are contrary to this dislocation.
And the sign of one who suffers dislocation to the anterior parts is that the knee swells according to the complement, but it cannot bend the part that is in pain. If forced to extend further towards the anterior parts, it cannot do so; and his urine is retained, and his swelling is an apostema. You must treat his mobility on the sole.
And the sign of one upon whom you have dislocated the parts behind is that he does not expand the groin, nor can that in front be bent which bends the one behind, and his thigh is shorter than the other, and the one behind is softened, and the hip bone is displaced at that location.
The main reduction of the species of this dislocation is that you consider, at once, if the dislocation is old and ancient, whether it has already hardened. If so, you administer the reduction according to its position as soon as possible; in such cases, this is not part of the entire treatment; then one should not conjecture about it further.
For the case where the dislocation is recent and belongs to one of the four species of dislocation, you should turn the joint back and extend it to the internal and external parts, and move it to the right and left, for it naturally returns and does not require further treatment.
Moreover, if it does not return in this manner, you should prepare a strong agent and extend the thigh of that side more, or on its legs, or apply linseed which is pressed over the groin; another agent should stretch the hand from above so that it touches the inner sides of the legs; then strongly apply linseed over the front of the thigh, and have another person hold the end of the linseed.
And it is the extension from his side inside to the angle at the side of the furcula, or from the back side to the side of the flank.
And it is the extension of all those in one go, so that the patient lies on the ground and arches his back.
And this type of extension is common to all four.
And therefore, if the dislocation returns as we have said, it will be well; otherwise, for every type, what I say about its own treatment is necessary.
In the reduction of its own, when dislocated, and the dislocation is on the inside parts, make sure the patient lies on his side; then place the cushion over the side in that location where between the hip bone and where the buttock is placed the angle; and extend the cushion above the part of the angle to the other side of the furcula.
And then another minister firmly takes hold of the legs, and presses the area around the hip on the affected side, extending to the outer parts by strong extension, so that it returns to its place.
And this species is of a milder cure for which that member is reduced compared to other species.
And if you excuse yourself and do not follow the training for this method of cure, then make sure that both feet of the patient are tied together firmly but gently, over both ankles and both insteps.
And let the length of each part be equal to the quantity of four fingers, and let the patient's thigh be extended more than the other by a quantity of two fingers.
And then place the patient's thigh over the top of the post, and let it be in the crook of the arm; and let the length from the ground be equal to the quantity of two braces.
And command a strong youth to lift the thigh, and place it over the patient's thigh. Another strong youth should lift the buttocks firmly, so that the joint returns to its place quickly.
And in the reduction proper, when dislocation is at the outer part, make sure the patient's thigh lies on a cushion according to what we have advised regarding the firmness; and strengthen the bandage over the patient's thigh and its ankle.
And then place two sticks, one on the foot and the other on the head; and place well-adjusted sticks in the middle of the leg placed over which wet cloths be wrapped, so that the patient is not damaged by the stick being between their thighs, for you must ensure the extension from the parts left behind is not pulled.
And then each minister extend to their part, and the doctor with his hand equalizes the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then may it go well that the leg be slightly extended, and this according to this narrative method for the leg; and the doctor places the palm of his right hand on the front of the injured leg, and approaches with the other hand, and at that approach, he holds the extended part at the back part to the front part.
And if the dislocation is at the rear parts, then do not cover the patient with a blanket leaving it open, and elevate it above the ground; having done this, place it on a hard cause, as when the width of the patient is dislocated to the rear parts, according to what we said about its position on two widths; and it should be over its face and the linens be extended, according to what we said, with no harm.
And a compression should be applied on the area where the joint is displaced.
Therefore, when the reduction is completed as it should — and you notice the sign of the reduction, which is that you extend two legs from the patient, and when you see them equal and the leg extends its usual space, then you know that the limb has been returned according to what is needed — then you place two cushions, apply padding, and wrap it with a bandage so that the width is not distorted in any part; and adhere the patient to rest for three or four days.
And then separate the thigh from the abdomen, comparing one hip to the other.
When you see them equal in circumference, you know that the dislocation is already fixed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any sign of swelling, then you return and apply a compress to that area, and extend it according to your initial structure, and leave it for three days.
Then return that, and allow it to heal gradually over each part for some days until it is comfortable.
Capitol..xxxii.
On the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder.
The shoulder can be dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated to the outward parts, to the inward parts, and to the front parts more than to the back parts; it is not dislocated to the rear parts.
And the sign of shoulder dislocation is that when you command the patient to apply their hand to their hip; then you know that the shoulder is dislocated.
And the restoration of all manners of it is that you make sure the patient lies straight, try to extend his back as much as possible; adhere your two hands to his face, and place his foot between your feet; then make sure to adhere the bottom of him with your palms, keep those between your own feet on the bottom of him with strength, and another minister extend his back so that the bottom returns to its place.
And the sign of relief is that his back adheres to the thigh with ease without effort.
And then apply that and adhere the back to the thigh, and place those simultaneously with a bandage for three or four days, and then remove those.
And do not use the patient in any labor for a few days until he is healed and comforted.
But if the reduction of it is excused to you, then administer strong extension to the limbs of which in the cure of the hip the previous narrative was given.
Capitol.xxxiii.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the thumb.
The thumb is sometimes reduced by slight movement, and sometimes dislocated according to need, and the dislocation of the thumb is performed either on the internal parts or on the external parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of the thumb is that you see the thumb swollen proceeding from the part to which it is dislocated.
More the treatment of the reduction of the thumb is by gentle stretching, and it should be extended easily and equalized so that it returns.
More in the treatment of the thumb when dislocated according to need, ensure that you make the patient lie down, and have a strong and brave assistant behind the side of the thumb in the middle of the location.
And then place your right hand on the foot of the upper part, and with your left hand hold the lower part of the foot at the location of the heel.
And then draw the foot to your right hand, and to your left by two passes, and extend your left hand; and push the foot on the right you lower the leg thereafter.
And do this by two passes as we have counted.
And then push the foot towards the leg for the third pass, and you are at the heel.
So if it returns in one or two passes according to this manner, and if you close the foot well, that is good; otherwise, then iterate the operation, for that does not return.
But if in you the reduction of it as we have recommended is prohibited, then make a cut on the outside of the foot, and stick a pointed rod into firm earth, and that should be between the patient's thighs, and you have already wrapped it with a cloth so that the wound does not get infected.
And then the minister should hold their thighs, and another minister extend the foot with their hands or with cloth that lies over the heel of the foot; and then each minister extend themselves against the extension of their side, and the rod should be extended between the patient's thighs holding it there, so that the body of the patient is not drawn to the sides you lower its extension.
And then the doctor equalizes the dislocation with his hands, while another assistant holds the healthy thigh at the parts left, so that the dislocation returns with ease.
And then, as the dislocation returns and the health of the reduction is declared to you, then apply a plaster strongly with wheat grains, and tie the foot with wheat grains to the leg.
And be careful of the nerves behind the talus, for the wheat over that area should not be strong and severely affect those nerves.
And leave it for two or three days; and if the wheat becomes moist, remove it.
And remove the wheat in the third day or in the fourth, and prohibit the patient from walking for forty days. But if it is necessary for the patient to walk before that time, then be sure that the dislocation is not dissolved and corrupted, and that it does not receive cure.
And if an abscess occurs at the site, then when you administer in the cavity of the area as mentioned in what preceded our narrative, in another location for treatment and fomentation, before it is suppurated.
Chapter XXXIV.
For the treatment of dislocation of the toes in the feet.
Make sure that which is dislocated is gently reduced within a small extent; and what is not difficult, before it is severe.
But if the dislocation is in some of the bones of the big toe, then make the patient lie on their side on the ground or on a table, and keep them in this position.
Then stand on the patient's side, and place your foot over what you suspect to be the joint, and gently press that area with your foot using its natural strength so that it may return to its proper position, and observe that which has been reduced and see that no swelling appears in that location.
And then place the foot of him on a table that can hold the whole foot, which has two ends, and strengthen it with structure decent and firm for three days; then release that and allow that rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and its recovery is assured.
Capitol.xxxv.
Of the species of displacement that are caused by inflammation or by fracture or by any combination thereof.
When any of those causes occur, and you are involved in the treatment of it and its restoration, you must ensure that the success leads to recovery.
And for this reason, you must not presume in the treatment of it; rather, one who is wise in the art, long accustomed to its use, merciful and pious, delayed, neither angry nor presumptuous, should begin with medicines that cauterize abscesses only, leaving the disease to divine judgment, but to him to whom you hope for a cure, for alleviation of the illness, in which some hope appears. And then make a reduction at the beginning of the cause as soon as possible, before it becomes an abscess.
And then, if the limb returns as we want, administer a regimen which prevents the formation of an abscess and heals the wound as is convenient with the use of drying ointments.
And if dislocation is accompanied by a fracture or injuries in the separated bones, then you reduce the joint, and do so as we have said in simple ailments previously discussed in those places.
And use and avoid according to your virtue that which is in the ways of fear according to what precedes my preaching; for you should give thanks to your Creator, who is more permanent for your glory, and pay more honors to your true self in your time. Thus you finish the words of Albucasim perfectly, for which may God be blessed. Amen.
